<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Alcoholic beverage</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><div class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint" id="mw-fr-revisiontag"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><span class="flaggedrevs-icon oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-articleSearch oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-iconWidget" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."></span><span class="fr-toggle-arrow oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicator-down oo-ui-indicatorElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-indicatorWidget" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" title="show/hide details"></span></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_details" id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Alcoholic_beverage">reviewed</a> on <i>6 October 2022</i>.</div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Drink with a substantial ethanol amount</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about drinks containing alcohol. For alcohol as a drug specifically and related health issues, see <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(drug)" title="Alcohol (drug)">Alcohol (drug)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Adult beverage" redirects here. Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Caffeinated_drink" title="Caffeinated drink">Caffeinated drink</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cannabis-infused_drink" title="Cannabis-infused drink">Cannabis-infused drink</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Common_alcoholic_beverages.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Common_alcoholic_beverages.jpg/290px-Common_alcoholic_beverages.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Common_alcoholic_beverages.jpg/435px-Common_alcoholic_beverages.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Common_alcoholic_beverages.jpg/580px-Common_alcoholic_beverages.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Common_alcoholic_beverages.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A selection of alcoholic drinks: <a href="/wiki/Red_wine" title="Red wine">red wine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malt_whisky" title="Malt whisky">malt whisky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lager" title="Lager">lager</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sparkling_wine" title="Sparkling wine">sparkling wine</a>, lager, cherry <a href="/wiki/Liqueur" title="Liqueur">liqueur</a> and red wine</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liquor_store_in_Breckenridge_Colorado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Liquor_store_in_Breckenridge_Colorado.jpg/290px-Liquor_store_in_Breckenridge_Colorado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Liquor_store_in_Breckenridge_Colorado.jpg/435px-Liquor_store_in_Breckenridge_Colorado.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Liquor_store_in_Breckenridge_Colorado.jpg/580px-Liquor_store_in_Breckenridge_Colorado.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liquor_store_in_Breckenridge_Colorado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Liquor_store" title="Liquor store">liquor store</a> in the United States. Global sales of alcoholic drinks exceeded $1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/10%5E12" title="10^12">trillion</a> in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IWSR_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-IWSR-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>An <b>alcoholic beverage</b> (also called an <b>alcoholic drink</b>, <b>adult beverage</b>, or simply a <b>drink</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Drink" title="Drink">drink</a> that contains <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, a type of <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(chemistry)" title="Alcohol (chemistry)">alcohol</a> that acts <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(drug)" title="Alcohol (drug)">as a drug</a> and is produced by <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermentation</a> of grains, fruits, or other sources of sugar. The consumption of alcoholic drinks, often referred to as "drinking", plays an important <a href="/wiki/Drinking_culture" title="Drinking culture">social role</a> in many cultures. Most countries have <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_law" title="Alcohol law">laws</a> regulating the production, sale, and consumption of alcoholic beverages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IARD_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-IARD-2">[2]</a></sup> Regulations may require the labeling of the percentage alcohol content (as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABV" title="ABV">ABV</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_proof" title="Alcohol proof">proof</a>) and the use of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_warning_label" title="Alcohol warning label">warning label</a>. <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_with_alcohol_prohibition" title="List of countries with alcohol prohibition">Some countries</a> <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">ban such activities entirely</a>, but alcoholic drinks are legal in most parts of the world. The global <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_industry" title="Alcohol industry">alcoholic drink industry</a> exceeded $1 trillion in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IWSR_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-IWSR-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alcohol is a <a href="/wiki/Depressant" title="Depressant">depressant</a>, which in low doses causes <a href="/wiki/Euphoria" title="Euphoria">euphoria</a>, reduces <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, and increases sociability. In higher doses, it causes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drunkenness" title="Drunkenness">drunkenness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stupor" title="Stupor">stupor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unconsciousness" title="Unconsciousness">unconsciousness</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a>. Long-term use can lead to an <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcohol use disorder</a>, an increased risk of developing <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_and_cancer" title="Alcohol and cancer">several types of cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_and_cardiovascular_disease" title="Alcohol and cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Physical_dependence" title="Physical dependence">physical dependence</a>.
</p><p>Alcohol is one of the most widely used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recreational_drug" title="Recreational drug">recreational drugs</a> in the world, and about 33% of all humans currently drink alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2018GBD_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2018GBD-3">[3]</a></sup> In 2015, among <a href="/wiki/Americans" title="Americans">Americans</a>, 86% of adults had consumed alcohol at some point, with 70% drinking it in the last year and 56% in the last month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niaaa_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-niaaa-4">[4]</a></sup> Alcoholic drinks are typically divided into three classes—<a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wines</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distilled_beverage" title="Distilled beverage">spirits</a>—and typically their alcohol content is between 3% and 50%.
</p><p>Discovery of late <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a> jugs suggest that intentionally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermented_drink" title="Fermented drink">fermented drinks</a> existed at least as early as the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 10,000 BC</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Several other animals are affected by alcohol similarly to humans and, once they consume it, will consume it again if given the opportunity, though humans are the only species known to produce alcoholic drinks intentionally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermented_drinks">Fermented drinks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Fermented drinks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lady_relaxing_next_to_glass_of_red_wine_and_glass_of_1664_beer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Lady_relaxing_next_to_glass_of_red_wine_and_glass_of_1664_beer.jpg/220px-Lady_relaxing_next_to_glass_of_red_wine_and_glass_of_1664_beer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Lady_relaxing_next_to_glass_of_red_wine_and_glass_of_1664_beer.jpg/330px-Lady_relaxing_next_to_glass_of_red_wine_and_glass_of_1664_beer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Lady_relaxing_next_to_glass_of_red_wine_and_glass_of_1664_beer.jpg/440px-Lady_relaxing_next_to_glass_of_red_wine_and_glass_of_1664_beer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lady_relaxing_next_to_glass_of_red_wine_and_glass_of_1664_beer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wine (left) and beer (right) are served in different glasses.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beer">Beer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Beer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">Beer</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beer_styles" title="Beer styles">Beer styles</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_beer_styles" title="List of beer styles">List of beer styles</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">Beer</a> is a beverage fermented from <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grain</a> <a href="/wiki/Mashing" title="Mashing">mash</a>. It is typically made from <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Mixture" title="Mixture">blend</a> of several grains and flavored with <a href="/wiki/Hops" title="Hops">hops</a>. Most beer is naturally carbonated as part of the fermentation process. If the fermented mash is distilled, then the drink becomes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distilled_beverage" title="Distilled beverage">spirit</a>. Beer is the most consumed alcoholic beverage in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_2005_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson_2005-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cider">Cider</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cider">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cider" title="Cider">Cider</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cider" title="Cider">Cider</a> or cyder (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/ər/: 'er' in 'letter'">ər</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">SY</span>-dər</i></a>) is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermented</a> alcoholic drink made from any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fruit_juice" title="Fruit juice">fruit juice</a>; <a href="/wiki/Apple_juice" title="Apple juice">apple juice</a> (traditional and most common), <a href="/wiki/Peach" title="Peach">peaches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pear" title="Pear">pears</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Perry" title="Perry">Perry</a>" cider) or other fruit. Cider alcohol content varies from 1.2% <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_by_volume" title="Alcohol by volume">ABV</a> to 8.5% or more in traditional English ciders. In some regions, cider may be called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apple_wine" title="Apple wine">apple wine</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermented_tea">Fermented tea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Fermented tea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fermented_tea" title="Fermented tea">Fermented tea</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fermented_tea" title="Fermented tea">Fermented tea</a> (also known as post-fermented tea or dark tea) is a class of <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a> that has undergone microbial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a>, from several months to many years. The tea leaves and the liquor made from them become darker with oxidation. Thus, the various kinds of fermented teas produced across <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> are also referred to as dark tea, not be confused with <a href="/wiki/Black_tea" title="Black tea">black tea</a>. The most famous fermented tea is <a href="/wiki/Kombucha" title="Kombucha">kombucha</a> which is often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homebrewed" title="Homebrewed">homebrewed</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pu-erh" title="Pu-erh">pu-erh</a>, produced in <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan Province</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haizhen,_Yang_Zhu_2008_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haizhen,_Yang_Zhu_2008-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hai-peng,_2013_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hai-peng,_2013-10">[10]</a></sup> and the Anhua dark tea produced in <a href="/wiki/Anhua_County" title="Anhua County">Anhua County</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunan_Province" title="Hunan Province">Hunan Province</a>. The majority of kombucha on the market are under 0.5% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABV" title="ABV">ABV</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermented_water">Fermented water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Fermented water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fermented water is an <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>-based water solution with approximately 15-17% ABV without <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweet_reserve" title="Sweet reserve">sweet reserve</a>. Fermented water is <i>exclusively</i> <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermented</a> with <a href="/wiki/White_sugar" title="White sugar">white sugar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yeast_(wine)" title="Yeast (wine)">yeast</a>, and water. Fermented water is clarified after the fermentation to produce a colorless or off-white liquid with no discernible taste other than that of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermented_sugar_water">Fermented sugar water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Fermented sugar water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Fermented sugar water is fermented water with added refined <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mead">Mead</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mead">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mead" title="Mead">Mead</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mead" title="Mead">Mead</a> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is an alcoholic drink made by fermenting honey with water, sometimes with various fruits, spices, grains, or hops. The alcoholic content of mead may range from as low as 3% ABV to more than 20%. The defining characteristic of mead is that the majority of the drink's fermentable sugar is derived from honey. Mead can also be referred to as "honeywine."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pulque">Pulque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Pulque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pulque" title="Pulque">Pulque</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pulque" title="Pulque">Pulque</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerican</a> fermented drink made from the "honey water" of <a href="/wiki/Maguey" title="Maguey">maguey</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Agave_americana" title="Agave americana">Agave americana</a></i>. The drink distilled from pulque is <a href="/wiki/Tequila" title="Tequila">tequila</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mezcal" title="Mezcal">mescal</a> Mezcal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rice_wine">Rice wine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Rice wine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rice_wine" title="Rice wine">Rice wine</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">Sake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huangjiu" title="Huangjiu">huangjiu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mijiu" title="Mijiu">mijiu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cheongju_(beverage)" title="Cheongju (beverage)">cheongju</a> are popular examples of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Asian" title="East Asian">East Asian</a> <a href="/wiki/Rice_wine" title="Rice wine">rice wine</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wine">Wine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Wine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">Wine</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">Wine</a> is a fermented beverage produced from <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grapes</a> and sometimes other fruits. Wine involves a longer fermentation process than beer and a long <a href="/wiki/Aging_of_wine" title="Aging of wine">aging process</a> (months or years), resulting in an alcohol content of 9%–16% <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_by_volume" title="Alcohol by volume">ABV</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Others">Others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fruit_wine" title="Fruit wine">Fruit wines</a> are made from fruits other than grapes, such as plums, cherries, or apples.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sparkling_wine" title="Sparkling wine">Sparkling wine</a> like French <a href="/wiki/Champagne" title="Champagne">Champagne</a>, Catalan <a href="/wiki/Cava_(Spanish_wine)" title="Cava (Spanish wine)">Cava</a> or Italian <a href="/wiki/Prosecco" title="Prosecco">Prosecco</a> can be made by means of a <a href="/wiki/Sparkling_wine#Secondary_fermentation" title="Sparkling wine">secondary fermentation</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distilled_beverages">Distilled beverages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Distilled beverages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liquor" title="Liquor">Liquor</a></div>
<p>Distilled beverages (also called liquors or spirit drinks) are alcoholic drinks produced by <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distilling</a> (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Concentration" title="Concentration">concentrating by distillation</a>) <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> produced by means of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermenting</a> grain, fruit, or vegetables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Unsweetened, distilled, alcoholic drinks that have an alcohol content of at least 20% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABV" title="ABV">ABV</a> are called <i>spirits</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> For the most common distilled drinks, such as whiskey and vodka, the alcohol content is around 40%. The term <i>hard liquor</i> is used in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> to distinguish distilled drinks from undistilled ones (implicitly weaker). <a href="/wiki/Vodka" title="Vodka">Vodka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gin" title="Gin">gin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baijiu" title="Baijiu">baijiu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dch%C5%AB" title="Shōchū">shōchū</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soju" title="Soju">soju</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tequila" title="Tequila">tequila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whisky" title="Whisky">whiskey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brandy" title="Brandy">brandy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rum" title="Rum">rum</a> are examples of distilled drinks. Distilling <a href="/wiki/Concentration" title="Concentration">concentrates</a> the alcohol and eliminates some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congener_(alcohol)" title="Congener (alcohol)">congeners</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeze_distillation" title="Freeze distillation">Freeze distillation</a> concentrates ethanol along with <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fusel_alcohol" title="Fusel alcohol">fusel alcohols</a> (fermentation by-products partially removed by distillation) in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Applejack_(beverage)" title="Applejack (beverage)">applejack</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fortified_wine" title="Fortified wine">Fortified wine</a> is wine, such as <a href="/wiki/Port_wine" title="Port wine">port</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sherry" title="Sherry">sherry</a>, to which a distilled beverage (usually <a href="/wiki/Brandy" title="Brandy">brandy</a>) has been added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALE_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ALE-14">[14]</a></sup> Fortified wine is distinguished from spirits made from wine in that spirits are produced by means of <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a>, while fortified wine is simply wine that has had a spirit added to it. Many different styles of fortified wine have been developed, including <a href="/wiki/Port_wine" title="Port wine">port</a>, sherry, <a href="/wiki/Madeira_wine" title="Madeira wine">madeira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marsala_wine" title="Marsala wine">marsala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commandaria" title="Commandaria">commandaria</a>, and the aromatized wine <a href="/wiki/Vermouth" title="Vermouth">vermouth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_pg_279_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_pg_279-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rectified_spirit">Rectified spirit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Rectified spirit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rectified_spirit" title="Rectified spirit">Rectified spirit</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rectified_spirit" title="Rectified spirit">Rectified spirit</a>, also called "neutral grain spirit", is alcohol which has been purified by means of "rectification" (i.e. repeated distillation). The term <i>neutral</i> refers to the spirit's lack of the flavor that would have been present if the mash ingredients had been distilled to a lower level of alcoholic purity. Rectified spirit also lacks any flavoring added to it after distillation (as is done, for example, with <a href="/wiki/Gin" title="Gin">gin</a>). Other kinds of spirits, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whiskey" title="Whiskey">whiskey</a>, are distilled to a lower alcohol percentage to preserve the flavor of the mash.
</p><p>Rectified spirit is a clear, colorless, flammable liquid that may contain as much as 95% <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_by_volume" title="Alcohol by volume">ABV</a>. It is often used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug" title="Pharmaceutical drug">medicinal purposes</a>. It may be a grain spirit or it may be made from other plants. It is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed_drinks" title="Mixed drinks">mixed drinks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liqueurs" title="Liqueurs">liqueurs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tincture" title="Tincture">tinctures</a>, and also as a household <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Congeners">Congeners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Congeners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congener_(alcohol)" title="Congener (alcohol)">Congener (alcohol)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wine_chemistry" title="Wine chemistry">Wine chemistry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hangover" title="Hangover">Hangover</a></div>
<p>In the alcoholic drinks industry, congeners are substances produced during <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermentation</a>. These substances include small amounts of chemicals such as occasionally desired other alcohols, like <a href="/wiki/Propanol" title="Propanol">propanol</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-methyl-1-butanol" title="3-methyl-1-butanol">3-methyl-1-butanol</a>, but also compounds that are never desired such as <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde" title="Acetaldehyde">acetaldehyde</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycols" title="Glycols">glycols</a>. Congeners are responsible for most of the taste and aroma of distilled alcoholic drinks, and contribute to the taste of non-distilled drinks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It has been suggested that these substances contribute to the symptoms of a <a href="/wiki/Hangover" title="Hangover">hangover</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tannins_in_wine" title="Tannins in wine">Tannins</a> are congeners found in wine in the presence of phenolic compounds. Wine tannins add bitterness, have a drying sensation, taste herbaceous and are often described as <a href="/wiki/Astringent" title="Astringent">astringent</a>. Wine tannins adds balance, complexity, structure and makes a wine last longer, so they play an important role in the <a href="/wiki/Aging_of_wine" title="Aging of wine">aging of wine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Amount_of_use">Amount of use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Amount of use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_alcohol_consumption_per_capita" title="List of countries by alcohol consumption per capita">List of countries by alcohol consumption per capita</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlcoholPerCapita.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="1011" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/AlcoholPerCapita.jpg/400px-AlcoholPerCapita.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/AlcoholPerCapita.jpg/600px-AlcoholPerCapita.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/AlcoholPerCapita.jpg/800px-AlcoholPerCapita.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlcoholPerCapita.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alcohol consumption per person in 2016. Consumption of alcohol is measured in liters of pure alcohol per person aged 15 or older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The average number of people who drink as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> was 39% for males and 25% for females (2.4 billion people in total).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2018GBD_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2018GBD-3">[3]</a></sup> Females on average drink 0.7 drinks per day while males drink 1.7 drinks per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2018GBD_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2018GBD-3">[3]</a></sup> The rates of drinking varies significantly in different areas of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2018GBD_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2018GBD-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reasons_for_use">Reasons for use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Reasons for use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For Historical use of wine or beer as sweetener/preservation in early <a href="/wiki/Evaporator_(marine)" title="Evaporator (marine)">desalinating sea water</a>, see <a href="/wiki/Grog#Background" title="Grog">Grog § Background</a>.</div>
<h3><span id="Ap.C3.A9ritifs_and_digestifs"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Apéritifs_and_digestifs">Apéritifs and digestifs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Apéritifs and digestifs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ap%C3%A9ritif_and_digestif" title="Apéritif and digestif">Apéritif and digestif</a></div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Ap%C3%A9ritif_and_digestif#Apéritifs" title="Apéritif and digestif">apéritif</a> is any alcoholic beverage usually served before a meal to stimulate the <a href="/wiki/Appetite" title="Appetite">appetite</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> while a <a href="/wiki/Ap%C3%A9ritif_and_digestif#Digestifs" title="Apéritif and digestif">digestif</a> is any alcoholic beverage served after a meal for the stated purpose of improving digestion. <a href="/wiki/Fortified_wine" title="Fortified wine">Fortified wine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liqueur" title="Liqueur">liqueurs</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Champagne_(wine)" title="Champagne (wine)">dry champagne</a> are common apéritifs. Because apéritifs are served before dining, they are usually dry rather than sweet. One example is <a href="/wiki/Cinzano" title="Cinzano">Cinzano</a>, a brand of <a href="/wiki/Vermouth" title="Vermouth">vermouth</a>. Digestifs include brandy, fortified wines and herb-infused spirits (<a href="/wiki/Drambuie" title="Drambuie">Drambuie</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caloric_content">Caloric content</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Caloric content">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USDA" title="USDA">USDA</a> uses a figure of 6.93 <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">kilocalories</a> (29.0 kJ) per gram of alcohol (5.47 kcal or 22.9 kJ per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millilitre" title="Millilitre">ml</a>) for calculating food energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distilled_spirit" title="Distilled spirit">distilled spirits</a>, a standard serving in the United States is 44 ml (1.5 US fl oz), which at 40% ethanol (80 <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_proof" title="Alcohol proof">proof</a>), would be 14 grams and 98 calories. For other than distille spirits, many alcoholic drinks contain carbohydrates, which adds to the calories per serving.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Alcoholic drinks are considered <a href="/wiki/Empty_calories" title="Empty calories">empty calorie</a> foods because other than food energy they contribute no essential nutrients. According to the U.S. Department of Agriculture, based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NHANES" title="NHANES">NHANES</a> 2013–2014 surveys, women in the US ages 20 and up consume on average 6.8 grams/day and men consume on average 15.5 grams/day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alcohol is known to potentiate the <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin response</a> of the human body to <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, which, in essence, "instructs" the body to convert consumed carbohydrates into fat and to suppress carbohydrate and fat oxidation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Ethanol is directly processed in the liver to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetyl_CoA" title="Acetyl CoA">acetyl CoA</a>, the same intermediate product as in glucose metabolism. Because ethanol is mostly metabolized and consumed by the liver, chronic excessive use can lead to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatty_liver" title="Fatty liver">fatty liver</a>. This leads to a chronic inflammation of the liver and eventually <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_liver_disease" title="Alcoholic liver disease">alcoholic liver disease</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flavoring">Flavoring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Flavoring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:R%C3%A9duction_du_vin_rouge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/R%C3%A9duction_du_vin_rouge.jpg/220px-R%C3%A9duction_du_vin_rouge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/R%C3%A9duction_du_vin_rouge.jpg/330px-R%C3%A9duction_du_vin_rouge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/R%C3%A9duction_du_vin_rouge.jpg/440px-R%C3%A9duction_du_vin_rouge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:R%C3%A9duction_du_vin_rouge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Reduction_(cooking)" title="Reduction (cooking)">Reduction</a> of red wine for a sauce by cooking it on a stovetop. It is called a reduction because the heat boils off some of the water and most of the more volatile alcohol, leaving a more concentrated, wine-flavoured sauce.</div></div></div>
<p>Pure ethanol tastes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitter_(taste)#Bitterness" title="Bitter (taste)">bitter</a> to humans; some people also describe it as sweet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> However, ethanol is also a moderately good <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> for many fatty substances and essential oils. This facilitates the use of flavoring and coloring compounds in alcoholic drinks as a taste mask, especially in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distilled_drinks" title="Distilled drinks">distilled drinks</a>. Some flavors may be naturally present in the beverage's raw material. Beer and wine may also be flavored before fermentation, and spirits may be flavored before, during, or after distillation. Sometimes flavor is obtained by allowing the beverage to stand for months or years in oak barrels, usually made of American or French oak. A few brands of spirits may also have fruit or herbs inserted into the bottle at the time of bottling.
</p><p>Wine is important in cuisine not just for its value as an accompanying beverage, but as a flavor agent, primarily in <a href="/wiki/Stock_(food)" title="Stock (food)">stocks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Braising" title="Braising">braising</a>, since its acidity lends balance to rich <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_taste#Savouriness" title="Basic taste">savory</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweet" title="Sweet">sweet</a> dishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wine_sauce" title="Wine sauce">Wine sauce</a> is an example of a culinary sauce that uses wine as a primary ingredient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Natural wines may exhibit a broad range of alcohol content, from below 9% to above 16% <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_by_volume" title="Alcohol by volume">ABV</a>, with most wines being in the 12.5–14.5% range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fortified_wine" title="Fortified wine">Fortified wines</a> (usually with brandy) may contain 20% alcohol or more.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcohol_measurement">Alcohol measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Alcohol measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcohol_concentration">Alcohol concentration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Alcohol concentration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left:15px; text-align:center">
<caption>Typical ABV ranges<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<td>Fruit juices
</td>
<td>&lt; 0.1%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Cider, wine coolers
</td>
<td>4%–8%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Beers
</td>
<td>typically 5% (range is from 3–15%)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Wines
</td>
<td>typically 13.5% (range is from 8%–17%)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Sakes
</td>
<td>15–16%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Fortified wines
</td>
<td>15–22%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Spirits
</td>
<td>typically 30%-40% (range is from 15% to, in some rare cases, up to 98%)
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_by_volume" title="Alcohol by volume">Alcohol by volume</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Concentration" title="Concentration">concentration</a> of alcohol in a beverage is usually stated as the percentage of <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_by_volume" title="Alcohol by volume">alcohol by volume</a>  (ABV, the number of milliliters (ml) of pure ethanol in 100 ml of beverage) or as <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_proof" title="Alcohol proof"><i>proof</i></a>. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <i>proof</i> is twice the percentage of alcohol by volume at 60 degrees Fahrenheit (e.g. 80 proof = 40% ABV). <i>Degrees proof</i> were formerly used in the United Kingdom, where 100 degrees proof was equivalent to 57.1% ABV. Historically, this was the most dilute spirit that would sustain the <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a>.
</p><p>Ordinary <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> cannot produce alcohol of more than 95.6% by weight, which is about 97.2% ABV (194.4 proof) because at that point alcohol is an <a href="/wiki/Azeotrope" title="Azeotrope">azeotrope</a> with water. A spirit which contains a very high level of alcohol and <i>does not contain any added flavoring</i> is commonly called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutral_grain_spirit" title="Neutral grain spirit">neutral spirit</a>. Generally, any distilled alcoholic beverage of 170 US proof or higher is considered to be a neutral spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeasts</a> cannot reproduce when the concentration of alcohol is higher than about 18%, so that is the practical limit for the strength of fermented drinks such as wine, beer, and <a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">sake</a>. However, some strains of yeast have been developed that can reproduce in solutions of up to 25% ABV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Serving_measures">Serving measures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Serving measures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_equivalence" title="Alcohol equivalence">Alcohol equivalence</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shot_sizes">Shot sizes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Shot sizes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Shot_glass#Sizes" title="Shot glass">Shot sizes</a> vary significantly from country to country. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, serving size in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Licensing_laws_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Licensing laws of the United Kingdom">licensed premises</a> is regulated under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weights_and_Measures_Acts_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Weights and Measures Acts of the United Kingdom">Weights and Measures Act (1985)</a>. A single serving size of spirits (<a href="/wiki/Gin" title="Gin">gin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whisky" title="Whisky">whisky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rum" title="Rum">rum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vodka" title="Vodka">vodka</a>) are sold in 25 ml or 35 ml quantities or multiples thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Beer is typically served in <a href="/wiki/Pint" title="Pint">pints</a> (568 ml), but is also served in half-pints or third-pints. In Israel, a single serving size of spirits is about twice as much, 50 or 60 mL.
</p><p>The shape of a glass can have a significant effect on how much one pours. A <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> study of students and bartenders' pouring showed both groups pour more into short, wide glasses than into tall, slender glasses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Aiming to pour one shot of alcohol (1.5 ounces or 44.3 ml), students on average poured 45.5 ml &amp; 59.6 ml (30% more) respectively into the tall and short glasses. The bartenders scored similarly, on average pouring 20.5% more into the short glasses. More experienced bartenders were more accurate, pouring 10.3% less alcohol than less experienced bartenders. Practice reduced the tendency of both groups to over pour for tall, slender glasses but not for short, wide glasses. These misperceptions are attributed to two perceptual biases: (1) Estimating that tall, slender glasses have more volume than shorter, wider glasses; and (2) Over focusing on the height of the liquid and disregarding the width.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_drinks">Standard drinks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Standard drinks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Standard_drink" title="Standard drink">Standard drink</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glass_of_whisky.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Glass_of_whisky.jpg/170px-Glass_of_whisky.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Glass_of_whisky.jpg/255px-Glass_of_whisky.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Glass_of_whisky.jpg/340px-Glass_of_whisky.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glass_of_whisky.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A "standard drink" of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_liquor" title="Hard liquor">hard liquor</a> does not necessarily reflect a typical serving size, such as seen here</div></div></div>
<p>There is no single standard, but a <i>standard drink</i> of 10g alcohol, which is used in the WHO AUDIT (Alcohol Use Disorders Identification Test)'s questionnaire form example,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_AUDIT_EN_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_AUDIT_EN-34">[34]</a></sup> have been adopted by more countries than any other amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalinowski2016_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalinowski2016-35">[35]</a></sup> 10 grams is equivalent to 12.7 millilitres.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Standard_drink#Pure_alcohol_measure" title="Standard drink">standard drink</a> is a notional drink that contains a specified amount of pure <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a>. The standard drink is used in many countries to quantify alcohol intake. It is usually expressed as a measure of beer, wine, or spirits. One standard drink always contains the same amount of alcohol regardless of serving size or the type of alcoholic beverage. The standard drink varies significantly from country to country. For example, it is 7.62 ml (6 grams) of alcohol in Austria, but in Japan it is 25 ml (19.75 grams).
</p>
<ul><li>In the United Kingdom, there is a system of <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_alcohol" title="Unit of alcohol">units of alcohol</a> which serves as a guideline for alcohol consumption. A single <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_alcohol" title="Unit of alcohol">unit of alcohol</a> is defined as 10 ml. The number of units present in a typical drink is sometimes printed on bottles. The system is intended as an aid to people who are regulating the amount of alcohol they drink; it is not used to determine serving sizes.</li>
<li>In the United States, the standard drink contains 0.6 US fluid ounces (18 ml) of alcohol. This is approximately the amount of alcohol in a 12-US-fluid-ounce (350 ml) glass of beer, a 5-US-fluid-ounce (150 ml) glass of wine, or a 1.5-US-fluid-ounce (44 ml) glass of a 40% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABV" title="ABV">ABV</a> (80 US proof) spirit.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws">Laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Legal_considerations"></span>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_laws" title="Alcohol laws">Alcohol laws</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drinking_age" title="Drinking age">Drinking age</a></div>
<p>Alcohol laws regulate the manufacture, packaging, labelling, distribution, sale, consumption, blood alcohol content of motor vehicle drivers, open containers, and transportation of alcoholic drinks. Such laws generally seek to reduce the adverse health and social impacts of alcohol consumption. In particular, alcohol laws set the <a href="/wiki/Legal_drinking_age" title="Legal drinking age">legal drinking age</a>, which usually varies between 15 and 21 years old, sometimes depending upon the type of alcoholic drink (e.g., beer vs wine vs hard liquor or distillates). Some countries do not have a legal drinking or purchasing age, but most countries set the minimum age at 18 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IARD_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-IARD-2">[2]</a></sup> Some countries, such as the U.S., have the drinking age higher than the legal age of majority (18), at age 21 in all 50 states. Such laws may take the form of permitting distribution only to licensed stores, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_monopoly" title="Alcohol monopoly">monopoly stores</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Pub" title="Pub">pubs</a> and they are often combined with taxation, which serves to reduce the demand for alcohol (by raising its price) and it is a form of revenue for governments. These laws also often limit the hours or days (e.g., "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_laws" title="Blue laws">blue laws</a>") on which alcohol may be sold or served, as can also be seen in the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_call_(bar_term)" title="Last call (bar term)">last call</a>" ritual in US and Canadian bars, where bartenders and servers ask patrons to place their last orders for alcohol, due to serving hour cutoff laws. In some countries, alcohol cannot be sold to a person who is already intoxicated. Alcohol laws in many countries prohibit <a href="/wiki/Drunk_driving" title="Drunk driving">drunk driving</a>.
</p><p>In some jurisdictions, alcoholic drinks are totally prohibited for reasons of <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> (e.g., Islamic countries with <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia law</a>) or for reasons of <a href="/wiki/Local_option" title="Local option">local option</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morals</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">Prohibition in the United States</a> from 1920 to 1933). In jurisdictions which enforce sharia law, the consumption of alcoholic drinks is an <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">illegal offense</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> although such laws may exempt non-Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alcoholic_beverage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_alcoholic_drinks" title="History of alcoholic drinks">History of alcoholic drinks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drinking_culture" title="Drinking culture">Drinking culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corps_Brunsviga_G%C3%B6ttingen_-_1837.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2539" data-file-width="2243" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Corps_Brunsviga_G%C3%B6ttingen_-_1837.jpg/220px-Corps_Brunsviga_G%C3%B6ttingen_-_1837.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Corps_Brunsviga_G%C3%B6ttingen_-_1837.jpg/330px-Corps_Brunsviga_G%C3%B6ttingen_-_1837.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Corps_Brunsviga_G%C3%B6ttingen_-_1837.jpg/440px-Corps_Brunsviga_G%C3%B6ttingen_-_1837.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corps_Brunsviga_G%C3%B6ttingen_-_1837.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of a <a href="/wiki/German_Student_Corps" title="German Student Corps">German Student Corps</a> (<a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brunswick" title="Duchy of Brunswick">Duchy of Brunswick</a>) shown drinking in a picture from 1837.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>10,000–5000 BC: Discovery of late <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a> jugs suggests that intentionally fermented drinks existed at least as early as the Neolithic period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>7000–5600 BC: Examination and analysis of ancient <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> jars from the neolithic village of <a href="/wiki/Jiahu" title="Jiahu">Jiahu</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a> province of northern China revealed residue left behind by the alcoholic drinks they had once contained. According to a study published in the <i><a href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_National_Academy_of_Sciences_of_the_United_States_of_America" title="Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America">Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences</a></i>, chemical analysis of the residue confirmed that a fermented drink made of grape and hawthorn fruit wine, honey mead and rice beer was being produced in 7000–5600 BC (McGovern et al., 2005; McGovern 2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgovern_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcgovern-40">[40]</a></sup> The results of this analysis were published in December 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li>9th–10th centuries AD: Medieval <a href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Alchemy and chemistry in the medieval Islamic world">Muslim chemists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Jabir_ibn_Hayyan" title="Jabir ibn Hayyan">Jābir ibn Ḥayyān</a> (Latin: Geber, ninth century) and <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_al-Razi" title="Abu Bakr al-Razi">Abū Bakr al-Rāzī</a> (Latin: Rhazes, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 865–925</span>) experimented extensively with the distillation of various substances. The distillation of wine is attested in Arabic works attributed to <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">al-Kindī</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 801</span>–873 CE) and to <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">al-Fārābī</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 872</span>–950), and in the 28th book of <a href="/wiki/Al-Zahrawi" title="Al-Zahrawi">al-Zahrāwī</a>'s (Latin: Abulcasis, 936–1013) <i>Kitāb al-Taṣrīf</i> (later translated into Latin as <i>Liber servatoris</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>12th century: The process of distillation spread from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> to Italy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habib_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-habib-43">[43]</a></sup> where distilled alcoholic drinks were recorded in the mid-12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes1970_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes1970-44">[44]</a></sup> In China, archaeological evidence indicates that the true distillation of alcohol began during the 12th century <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Song_dynasty" title="Southern Song dynasty">Southern Song</a> dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haw_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-haw-45">[45]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Still" title="Still">still</a> has been found at an archaeological site in Qinglong, <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a>, dating to the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haw_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-haw-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>14th century: In India, the true distillation of alcohol was introduced from the Middle East, and was in wide use in the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> by the 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habib_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-habib-43">[43]</a></sup> By the early 14th century, distilled alcoholic drinks had spread throughout the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">European continent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes1970_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes1970-44">[44]</a></sup></li></ul>
